# Rank Hayday core features

> I am making a vertical slice of the game Hayday for mobile, give me the core features ranked most critical to the gameplay experience.

For a mobile Hay Day vertical slice, prioritize the loop that makes farming satisfying—not the full feature set.

| Rank | Core feature | Why it matters | Vertical-slice scope |
|---:|---|---|---|
| 1 | Plant → wait → harvest loop | The game’s central tactile and emotional loop | 2–3 crops, drag-to-plant, growth timers, satisfying harvest feedback |
| 2 | Production machines | Converts basic crops into valuable goods and creates planning decisions | One machine, such as a bakery, with 2–3 recipes and a production queue |
| 3 | Order fulfillment | Gives production a purpose and drives progression | Simple order board with rotating requests and coin/XP rewards |
| 4 | Farm economy | Connects every action through scarcity and rewards | Coins, item costs, sale values, and carefully balanced production times |
| 5 | Progression and unlocking | Creates short-term goals and the desire to continue | XP, 3–5 levels, and unlocks for one crop, recipe, or field |
| 6 | Inventory management | Forces meaningful choices about what to grow and produce | Limited barn storage with a clear capacity indicator; skip upgrades initially |
| 7 | Farm layout and interaction | Makes the farm feel personal and physically understandable | Fixed or lightly editable plots, one production building, tap/drag controls |
| 8 | Juice and presentation | Much of Hay Day’s appeal comes from warmth and responsiveness | Characterful animation, particles, sound, bouncing rewards, readable timers |
| 9 | Guided onboarding | Farming systems are simple individually but can overwhelm when introduced together | A 2–3 minute tutorial covering planting, harvesting, baking, and orders |
| 10 | Return-session design | Timers only work when returning feels rewarding | Persist growth/production timers and show offline completion on relaunch |

The essential playable sequence should be:

**Plant wheat → harvest wheat → make bread → complete an order → earn coins and XP → unlock something → repeat.**

For the slice, I would deliberately exclude:

- Multiplayer and neighborhoods
- Roadside shop and player trading
- Animals, fishing, mining, and town systems
- Large-scale decoration
- Multiple currencies
- Random reward systems
- Complex barn/silo upgrades
- Live events and monetization

A strong vertical-slice target is a polished **10–15 minute first session**, followed by one meaningful return a few minutes later. If ranks 1–5 feel good, the project already communicates the heart of Hay Day.

> Crops loop:
> Tap the crop, gives a sickle visual, Drag the sickle over grown crops to harvest them, they get cut off and are placed onto the ground before being collected.(Drops Xp)

I’ve cleaned this into a working feature specification and will treat it as the baseline scope for the vertical slice.

# Hay Day Vertical Slice — Feature Notes

## 1. Crop harvesting

- Tap a mature crop to equip/show the sickle.
- Drag the sickle across mature crop patches to harvest them.
- Harvested crops are visibly cut and dropped onto the ground.
- The dropped crops are then collected into storage.
- Harvesting grants XP.

## 2. Farm patches

- Farm patches are available from the build menu.
- Drag patches from the menu into the farm and place them in valid locations.
- Crops can only be planted on empty farm patches.

## 3. Planting crops

- Tap an empty farm patch to open the available seed selection.
- Drag a seed type across empty patches to plant automatically.
- Each planted patch consumes one corresponding seed.
- Crops grow on a timer.
- Growth can be completed early using the speed-up system.

## 4. Animal pen and purchasing animals

- The animal pen contains the farm’s animals.
- Tap the pen to display the animals available for purchase.
- Drag an animal into the pen to purchase and place it.
- The number of animals determines how much the pen can consume and produce.

### Feeding animals

- Tap the pen to display valid feed options.
- Drag feed onto the pen to feed the animals.
- Feeding begins a production timer.
- Production can be completed early using the speed-up system.
- Once production finishes, the pen can be harvested.
- More animals allow more units to be fed and harvested per production cycle.

## 5. Feed mill

### Construction

- Open the build menu and drag the feed mill onto the farm.
- Position it in a valid location.
- Construction takes 15 seconds.
- Construction can be sped up.

### Production

- Tap the completed feed mill to open its production options.
- The vertical slice includes chicken feed.
- Producing feed consumes the required ingredients.
- Feed production takes time and can be sped up.

## 6. Bakery

- The bakery unlocks at level 4.
- Tap the bakery to access its recipes.
- The vertical slice includes bread.
- Bread consumes wheat.
- Baking takes time and can be sped up.

## 7. Request board and truck deliveries

- Orders periodically appear on the request board.
- Each order displays its required items and rewards.
- The player can submit an order if all required items are available.
- Submitting removes the items from storage.
- The truck leaves to deliver the order.
- When the truck returns, the player taps it to claim the rewards.
- Rewards can include coins and XP.

## 8. Visitors

- Visitors can arrive at the farm and request to buy items.
- The player has three response options:

  - **Wait a moment:** Keep the visitor and request active.
  - **No, sorry:** Reject the request and dismiss the visitor.
  - **Yes, here you go:** Sell the requested items and receive the displayed reward.

## 9. Progression and leveling

- Reward-producing activities grant XP.
- Reaching an XP threshold increases the player’s level.
- Leveling up unlocks new buildings, recipes, and activities.

### Planned unlocks

- **Level 1:** Chickens and farm patches are already available.
- **Level 4:** Unlock bread and the bakery; award one gem and coins.

## 10. Storage

The player has two independent storage buildings.

### Silo

- Stores grain and vegetables.
- Starts with a maximum capacity of 50 items.

### Barn

- Stores feed, animal products, tools, building materials, and other items.
- Starts with a maximum capacity of 50 items.

### Storage upgrades

- The silo and barn have separate capacity limits.
- Storage capacity can be increased using upgrade materials.
- Planned materials:

  - Nails
  - Screws
  - Wood panels

## 11. Visiting neighbors

- The player can visit a neighboring farm.
- The neighbor has a roadside shop containing tools and other purchasable items.
- Daily gift baskets can be opened for rewards.

## 12. Farm naming

- The player can choose a name for their farm.
- The chosen name should be saved and displayed in relevant farm UI.

## Shared speed-up system

The following timers can be completed early:

- Crop growth
- Feed mill construction
- Feed production
- Bakery production
- Animal production

The vertical slice should use one consistent speed-up interaction and currency treatment across these systems.

## Primary gameplay loop

**Build patches → plant wheat → harvest wheat → mill chicken feed → feed chickens → collect animal products → bake bread → fulfill orders or visitor requests → earn coins and XP → unlock new content.**

The biggest scope risk is the neighbor/roadside-shop system because it introduces a second farm, shop inventory, daily rewards, navigation, and additional persistence. If time becomes tight, it should be the first major feature deferred after the primary production-and-order loop is polished.

> Give me a list of isometric sprites i will need to get me going.

Use one consistent isometric grid first—ideally a **2:1 diamond tile**, with every building anchored at the center of its footprint.

## World item sprites

Create small isometric versions for objects that can appear physically in the world:

- Wheat
- Wheat seed bag
- Chicken feed

- XP star

- Screw

The inventory can use larger, cleaner icon versions derived from these sprites.

## Useful environment fillers

These are not mechanically essential, but a few will stop the farm from feeling empty:

- Small grass tufts
- Flowers
- Pebbles
- Bushes
- Small tree
- Large tree
- Tree stump
- Wooden crate
- Barrel
- Hay bale
- Small pond or water tile

## Effects and state indicators

- Building construction dust
- Construction-complete burst
- Speed-up sparkle
- Production progress indicator
- Production-ready bounce or glow
- Locked-building overlay
- Level-up burst
- Item collection arc
- Insufficient-space indicator
- Insufficient-items indicator

## Recommended production order

1. Grass tile and placement grid
2. Farm patch and wheat growth stages
3. Sickle, harvesting, wheat drop, and collection effects
4. Chicken pen, chicken, feed, and egg
5. Feed mill and its construction/production states
6. Silo and barn
7. Request board, road, and truck
8. Bakery and bread
9. Visitor
10. Neighbor shop and gift basket
11. Farmhouse and decorative assets

A practical first milestone is approximately **35–45 unique sprites**, excluding animation frames: terrain, wheat loop, one chicken, the pen, feed mill, storage buildings, request board, truck, resources, and basic effects. That is enough to make the main loop visually playable before investing in decorative polish.

> Give me a spec for the isometric tiles that i can keep the art generic prompt bound to that will keep everything aligned and the same size.

# Isometric Art Specification

Use this as the fixed technical specification for every generated asset. Only change the subject, footprint, animation state, and visual style description.

## 1. Master canvas

- **Canvas:** 1024 × 1024 px
- **Format:** PNG with transparency
- **Color:** sRGB
- **Projection:** Orthographic 2:1 isometric
- **Camera:** Fixed; no perspective or lens distortion
- **Ground anchor:** `(512, 768)`
- **Normalized pivot:** `(0.5, 0.75)`
- **Tile size:** 256 × 128 px
- **Vertical world unit:** 128 px
- **Rendering scale:** Generate at 1024 px, then downscale consistently if needed

Every asset uses this same canvas and anchor, including crops, animals, buildings, and ground items.

## 2. Base tile alignment

A 1 × 1 tile is a diamond centered on `(512, 768)`:

| Point | Coordinate |
|---|---:|
| Back/top | `(512, 704)` |
| Right | `(640, 768)` |
| Front/bottom | `(512, 832)` |
| Left | `(384, 768)` |

The tile is therefore:

- 256 px wide
- 128 px tall
- Oriented at approximately ±26.565°
- Never rotated differently between assets

The two visible front-facing sides are the bottom-left and bottom-right sides.

## 3. Footprint sizes

Keep every footprint centered on the ground anchor.

| Footprint | Ground width | Ground depth | Suggested use |
|---|---:|---:|---|
| 1 × 1 | 256 px | 128 px | Crops, animals, crates, small props |
| 2 × 2 | 512 px | 256 px | Feed mill, bakery, silo |
| 3 × 3 | 768 px | 384 px | Barn, large animal pen |
| 2 × 1 | Use grid guide | Use grid guide | Board, stalls, narrow structures |

Buildings may rise upward from the footprint but must not move the ground anchor.

Do not let generated artwork touch the canvas edge. Preserve at least 48 px of transparent padding.

## 4. Scale rules

Use these limits to prevent inconsistent object sizes:

- **Single crop:** Up to 40% of a 1 × 1 tile’s width
- **Crop filling a patch:** Up to 85% of the tile footprint
- **Ground item:** 25–40% of a tile
- **Chicken:** 45–60% of a tile’s width
- **Visitor:** Approximately 190–220 px tall
- **Small prop:** No more than one tile
- **2 × 2 building:** Occupies 80–90% of its footprint
- **3 × 3 building:** Occupies 80–90% of its footprint
- **Building height:** Normally 350–600 px above the anchor

Doors, windows, feed bags, characters, and tools should follow the same apparent scale across every sprite.

## 5. Direction convention

Lock these directions for all assets:

- **Back-left:** Upper-left
- **Back-right:** Upper-right
- **Front-left:** Lower-left
- **Front-right:** Lower-right
- **Vertical:** Perfectly vertical on the canvas

The primary presentation angle should show the object’s **front-left and front-right faces**.

Never mirror individual buildings unless their lighting, signs, doors, and animation frames are also corrected.

## 6. Lighting and shadows

- Main light comes from the **upper-left/front-left**
- Shadows fall toward the **lower-right**
- Use soft ambient shadows
- Avoid dramatic or cinematic lighting
- Keep brightness and color temperature consistent
- Do not include a large painted background shadow
- Keep contact shadows inside or immediately around the footprint

Ideally, export shadows separately:

- `asset_body.png`
- `asset_shadow.png`

If using a combined sprite, ensure the shadow does not alter its alignment.

## 7. Asset-state rules

All states of the same object must:

- Use the same 1024 × 1024 canvas
- Use the same `(512, 768)` pivot
- Retain the same footprint
- Retain the same camera
- Retain the same lighting
- Avoid shifting, resizing, or rotating between frames

For example, every wheat growth stage must remain planted at exactly the same ground position.

### Standard building states

- Placement ghost
- Construction foundation
- Under construction
- Completed and idle
- Working
- Production ready
- Selected/highlighted

### Standard crop states

- Empty tilled patch
- Newly planted
- Early growth
- Middle growth
- Fully grown
- Being harvested
- Cut/harvested
- Dropped crop bundle

### Standard animal states

- Idle
- Walking
- Eating
- Producing
- Ready to harvest

## 8. Generic reusable prompt

```text
Create a clean isometric mobile farming-game sprite of [SUBJECT].

STATE:
[STATE OR ANIMATION FRAME]

FOOTPRINT:
[1x1, 2x2, 3x3, or WIDTH x DEPTH] isometric tiles.

STRICT TECHNICAL SPECIFICATION:
Use a fixed orthographic 2:1 isometric projection with no perspective distortion.
The final image must be exactly 1024x1024 pixels with a transparent background.
One isometric tile is exactly 256 pixels wide and 128 pixels tall.
Center the object's ground footprint at pixel coordinate 512,768.
Treat coordinate 512,768 as the permanent ground anchor and pivot.
The object must rise vertically upward from this anchor.
Show the front-left and front-right faces.
The isometric horizontal axes must remain at approximately plus and minus
26.565 degrees.
Do not rotate, resize, shift, crop, or change the camera.
Maintain at least 48 pixels of transparent padding around the visible artwork.

LIGHTING:
Use soft, consistent lighting from the upper-left.
Use a subtle contact shadow falling toward the lower-right.
Avoid dramatic lighting, perspective, depth of field, or environmental background.

SCALE:
The object must occupy approximately 85 percent of its specified ground
footprint while remaining consistent with other assets generated from this
specification.

STYLE:
[LOCKED VISUAL STYLE DESCRIPTION]

The sprite must contain only the requested subject and its contact shadow.
No interface elements, labels, text, tile grid, scenery, border, background,
platform, floating island, or decorative frame.
```

## 9. Negative prompt

Keep this unchanged for every asset:

```text
perspective camera, three-point perspective, top-down view, side view,
front view, camera tilt, camera rotation, different isometric angle,
fisheye, lens distortion, horizon, landscape, background, sky,
floating island, circular platform, square platform, pedestal,
UI, text, label, border, frame, cropped object, off-center object,
inconsistent scale, oversized object, tiny object, hard cinematic shadow,
dramatic lighting, depth of field, motion blur, watermark
```

## 10. Example: feed mill

```text
Create a clean isometric mobile farming-game sprite of a small wooden feed
mill with a grain hopper, mixing drum, chute, and turning wheel.

STATE:
Completed and idle.

FOOTPRINT:
2x2 isometric tiles.

[Insert the unchanged strict technical specification.]

STYLE:
Friendly stylized 3D mobile-game artwork, rounded shapes, readable silhouette,
slightly exaggerated proportions, warm farm colors, hand-painted texture,
moderate detail, clean edges, soft shading, and no visual noise.
```

## 11. File naming

Use predictable names:

```text
category_subject_state_direction_frame.png
```

Examples:

```text
crop_wheat_growth_01.png
crop_wheat_growth_04.png
building_feed_mill_idle.png
building_feed_mill_working_01.png
animal_chicken_eating_front_right_01.png
item_chicken_feed_ground.png
```

## Important limitation

A text prompt alone will not guarantee pixel-perfect placement. The most dependable workflow is to place every generated sprite over a reusable 1024 × 1024 alignment guide, reposition it so the footprint matches the guide, and export it with the fixed `(512, 768)` pivot. Generate consistently, but enforce alignment during export and in the game engine.

> Now lets get the high level architecting nailed down.
>
> We have TileRegistrySO, it contains a list of Tile class, The tile class has the sprite reference and a category.
>
> We are going to be using a factory pattern to build tile functionality. We simple components that can be re used.
>
> Each factory is itself a ScriptableObject.
> TileFactorySO will have in input for the TIleRegistry, from the tile registry it gets the desires tile, creates it, places it on the grid at the appopriate spot.
>
> For the Farm Patch Tile it will call to that base tile factory function and with the return it will add on a functionality to click to open a context menu.
> This context menu will be how we put in options like the Sickle and seed and things in the future.

<details><summary>2 previous messages</summary>

> I’ll map this onto the project’s existing Unity structure, then turn it into clear responsibilities and object flow. The key design point is keeping tile creation, placement, and runtime behavior separate enough that future crops, buildings, and tools don’t force factory rewrites.
>
> <details><summary>Ran 2 commands</summary>
>
> - Ran `rg --files -g "*.cs" -g "*.asmdef" -g "*.asset" -g "*.unity" | Select-Object -First 250`
> - Ran `Get-ChildItem -Force`
>
> </details>
</details>

Your direction is sound. The main architectural adjustment I recommend is: **the registry stores definitions, the build service coordinates placement, factories assemble objects, and components provide runtime behavior.**

This prevents `TileFactorySO` from becoming responsible for lookup, placement rules, interaction, persistence, and gameplay logic all at once.

## High-level structure

```mermaid
flowchart LR
    UI["Build Menu"] --> BS["TileBuildService"]
    BS --> R["TileRegistrySO"]
    BS --> G["GridService"]
    BS --> F["TileFactorySO"]
    F --> TI["TileInstance"]
    F --> C["Reusable Components"]

    TI --> I["Interaction Router"]
    I --> CAS["ContextActionSource"]
    CAS --> AP["Action Providers"]
    AP --> CM["Context Menu"]
    CM --> TC["Tool Controller"]
```

## 1. Tile definitions

I would rename the current `Tile` class to `TileDefinitionSO`. `Tile` can become confusing alongside Unity’s own tile classes.

```csharp
[CreateAssetMenu(menuName = "Farm/Tiles/Tile Definition")]
public sealed class TileDefinitionSO : ScriptableObject
{
    [SerializeField] private string tileId;
    [SerializeField] private Sprite sprite;
    [SerializeField] private TileCategory category;
    [SerializeField] private Vector2Int footprint = Vector2Int.one;
    [SerializeField] private TileFactorySO factory;

    public string TileId => tileId;
    public Sprite Sprite => sprite;
    public TileCategory Category => category;
    public Vector2Int Footprint => footprint;
    public TileFactorySO Factory => factory;
}
```

Each definition identifies:

- What the tile is
- How it looks
- How much grid space it occupies
- Which factory assembles its runtime functionality

The category should primarily be used for filtering and presentation. Avoid large behavior switches such as:

```csharp
switch (definition.Category)
```

The factory reference already determines the behavior.

## 2. Tile registry

```csharp
[CreateAssetMenu(menuName = "Farm/Tiles/Tile Registry")]
public sealed class TileRegistrySO : ScriptableObject
{
    [SerializeField] private List<TileDefinitionSO> tiles;

    public bool TryGet(string tileId, out TileDefinitionSO definition);
}
```

Responsibilities:

- Store every tile definition
- Resolve a stable tile ID into its definition
- Detect duplicate IDs during validation
- Provide category-filtered lists to the build menu

It should not create tiles or contain runtime state.

Use stable IDs such as:

```text
terrain.grass
terrain.farm_patch
building.feed_mill
building.bakery
animal_pen.chicken
```

These IDs can also be saved to persistence later.

## 3. Tile build service

The registry dependency belongs here rather than inside every factory.

```csharp
public sealed class TileBuildService : MonoBehaviour
{
    [SerializeField] private TileRegistrySO registry;
    [SerializeField] private GridService grid;

    public TileBuildResult TryBuild(TileBuildRequest request);
}
```

A request contains:

```csharp
public readonly struct TileBuildRequest
{
    public readonly string TileId;
    public readonly Vector2Int GridPosition;
    public readonly TileOrientation Orientation;
}
```

The build sequence should be:

1. Resolve the definition through `TileRegistrySO`.
2. Ask the grid whether the footprint can be placed.
3. Ask the definition’s factory to create and configure the tile.
4. Commit the occupied cells to the grid.
5. Publish a `TilePlaced` event.
6. Return the created `TileInstance`.

If any step fails, the grid should remain unchanged.

## 4. Base factory

Use the template-method pattern. The public build flow stays consistent while specialized factories only add components.

```csharp
public abstract class TileFactorySO : ScriptableObject
{
    public TileInstance Create(
        TileDefinitionSO definition,
        TileBuildContext context)
    {
        TileInstance instance = CreateBaseTile(definition, context);
        Configure(instance, definition, context);
        return instance;
    }

    protected virtual TileInstance CreateBaseTile(
        TileDefinitionSO definition,
        TileBuildContext context)
    {
        // Create root GameObject.
        // Add SpriteRenderer and collider.
        // Add TileInstance and GridOccupant.
        // Apply sprite, position, sorting, and footprint.
    }

    protected abstract void Configure(
        TileInstance instance,
        TileDefinitionSO definition,
        TileBuildContext context);
}
```

The factory should create and configure the object, but the build service should own grid validation and placement commitment.

Factories are shared ScriptableObject assets. They must remain stateless:

- Do not store the last created tile.
- Do not store current grid coordinates.
- Do not store timers or selection state.
- Pass runtime dependencies through `TileBuildContext`.

## 5. Runtime tile

Every created tile gets a common root component.

```csharp
public sealed class TileInstance : MonoBehaviour
{
    public TileDefinitionSO Definition { get; private set; }
    public Vector2Int GridPosition { get; private set; }
    public TileOrientation Orientation { get; private set; }

    public void Initialize(
        TileDefinitionSO definition,
        Vector2Int position,
        TileOrientation orientation);
}
```

Common base components:

- `TileInstance`
- `GridOccupant`
- `SpriteRenderer`
- Appropriate collider
- Optional sorting component

Specialized factories then add gameplay components.

## 6. Farm patch factory

```csharp
[CreateAssetMenu(menuName = "Farm/Tiles/Factories/Farm Patch")]
public sealed class FarmPatchTileFactorySO : TileFactorySO
{
    protected override void Configure(
        TileInstance instance,
        TileDefinitionSO definition,
        TileBuildContext context)
    {
        GameObject target = instance.gameObject;

        target.AddComponent<PointerInteractable>();
        target.AddComponent<ContextActionSource>();

        var patch = target.AddComponent<FarmPatchState>();
        patch.Initialize(context.TimeService);

        var planting = target.AddComponent<PlantActionProvider>();
        planting.Initialize(context.InventoryService);

        var harvesting = target.AddComponent<HarvestActionProvider>();
        harvesting.Initialize(context.InventoryService);
    }
}
```

This creates a tile through the common base path and then attaches reusable farm-patch functionality.

## 7. Context menu architecture

Do not make the farm patch itself hardcode a list containing “Sickle,” “Wheat Seed,” and future actions.

Instead, let components contribute actions through a common interface:

```csharp
public interface IContextActionProvider
{
    void CollectActions(ContextActionCollection actions);
}
```

`ContextActionSource` collects available actions from sibling components:

```csharp
public sealed class ContextActionSource : MonoBehaviour
{
    public IReadOnlyList<ContextAction> GetAvailableActions();
}
```

Example providers:

- `PlantActionProvider`
- `HarvestActionProvider`
- `FeedActionProvider`
- `CollectProductActionProvider`
- `SpeedUpActionProvider`
- `MoveBuildingActionProvider`
- `SellActionProvider`

This makes the menu state-dependent:

- Empty patch → seed actions
- Growing crop → crop information and speed-up
- Mature crop → sickle/harvest action
- Invalid inventory state → disabled seed action
- Building under construction → speed-up action

## 8. Tap and tool flow

The interaction path should be:

1. Player taps a collider.
2. `PointerInteractable` reports the selected object.
3. The interaction controller finds its `ContextActionSource`.
4. The source gathers currently available actions.
5. The context-menu presenter renders those actions.
6. Selecting an action activates the appropriate tool or executes an immediate command.

For the sickle:

```text
Tap mature patch
→ HarvestActionProvider offers Harvest
→ Player selects Harvest
→ ToolController activates SickleTool
→ Drag raycasts across patches
→ SickleTool calls IHarvestable.TryHarvest()
```

For planting:

```text
Tap empty patch
→ PlantActionProvider offers available seeds
→ Player selects wheat
→ ToolController activates PlantSeedTool
→ Drag crosses empty patches
→ Tool consumes one seed and plants each valid patch
```

This is important because the sickle and seeds are active tools, not permanent responsibilities of the farm-patch tile.

## 9. Reusable component set

A strong initial component library would be:

### Common tile components

- `TileInstance`
- `GridOccupant`
- `PointerInteractable`
- `ContextActionSource`
- `Buildable`
- `Movable`

### Farm components

- `FarmPatchState`
- `Plantable`
- `Growable`
- `Harvestable`
- `ProductionQueue`
- `FeedConsumer`
- `ProductCollector`
- `ConstructionTimer`

### Context action providers

- `PlantActionProvider`
- `HarvestActionProvider`
- `FeedActionProvider`
- `CollectActionProvider`
- `SpeedUpActionProvider`
- `MoveActionProvider`

Each component should represent one capability. Factories decide which capabilities a tile receives.

## 10. Persistence boundary

Save data should reference definitions and state, not Unity object references:

```csharp
[Serializable]
public sealed class PlacedTileSaveData
{
    public string TileId;
    public Vector2Int GridPosition;
    public TileOrientation Orientation;
    public string RuntimeStateJson;
}
```

A farm patch’s runtime state would include:

- Crop ID
- Planting timestamp
- Growth completion timestamp
- Current growth state

On load, the build service reconstructs the tile through its factory and then applies its saved runtime state.

## Final responsibility split

| System | Responsibility |
|---|---|
| `TileDefinitionSO` | Static tile data and factory selection |
| `TileRegistrySO` | Tile-definition lookup |
| `TileBuildService` | Validation and placement orchestration |
| `GridService` | Occupancy and coordinate conversion |
| `TileFactorySO` | Common object construction |
| Specialized factory | Component composition |
| `TileInstance` | Runtime identity and grid location |
| Gameplay components | Individual capabilities and state |
| `ContextActionSource` | Discover available interactions |
| Context-menu presenter | Display actions |
| `ToolController` | Handle drag-based sickle, seed, and similar tools |

The central rule is: **factories assemble capabilities, components implement capabilities, and the context menu discovers capabilities.** This will carry cleanly from farm patches into animal pens, production buildings, storage, and construction without category-specific monoliths.

Key entry points:

- [TileDefinitionSO.cs](./Assets/Scripts/Tiles/Data/TileDefinitionSO.cs)

- [ToolController.cs](./Assets/Scripts/Tools/ToolController.cs)

The implementation includes:

- Singleton-backed registry access
- Stable tile IDs and category filtering
- Isometric coordinate conversion and multi-cell occupancy
- Transactional tile placement
- Base and farm-patch ScriptableObject factories
- Runtime tile identity and save DTOs
- Click/context-action discovery
- Stateless ScriptableObject tools
- Sickle/harvest tool
- Drag-tool input routing
- Empty, growing, and mature farm-patch states
- Plant, harvest, and speed-up action providers
- Offline-compatible UTC growth timestamps
- Your guarded `verbose` logging convention

Initial Unity setup:

1. Create a `TileRegistrySO`.
2. Create `DefaultTileFactorySO` and `FarmPatchTileFactorySO` assets.
3. Create tile definitions and add them to the registry.
4. Add `RegistryService`, `GridService`, `TileBuildService`, `InteractionController`, `ContextMenuController`, and `ToolController` to a scene systems object.
5. Assign the registry to `RegistryService`.
6. Add an EventSystem and `Physics2DRaycaster` to the world camera.
7. Import isometric sprites with custom pivot `(0.5, 0.75)` and 100 pixels per unit.
8. Have your menu UI subscribe to `ContextMenuController.Opened` and `Closed`.

Example placement:

```csharp
TileBuildResult result = TileBuildService.instance.TryBuild(
    new TileBuildRequest(
        "terrain.farm_patch",
        new Vector2Int(0, 0)));
```

The scripts passed a compile-style check with zero errors. The remaining warnings are expected from inspector-assigned fields and `const bool verbose = false`. A final Unity Editor compilation is still needed once this folder is placed inside the full Unity project.

Implemented the full tutorial harvest loop.

- [UserModel.cs](./Assets/Scripts/User/UserModel.cs:4) is automatically created and persists between scenes.
- Barn and grain storage each start with 50 capacity.
- [ItemStorageModel.cs](./Assets/Scripts/User/ItemStorageModel.cs:4) supports capacity limits, adding, removing, querying quantities, upgrades, and change events.

[TutorialSetup.cs](./Assets/Scripts/TutorialSetup.cs:40) now:

- Automatically creates a compact 3×2 layout of six patches.
- Starts every patch with mature wheat.
- Reuses existing farm patches at those grid positions.
- Still supports pressing `T` as a manual fallback.
- Is explicitly configured in [Main.unity](./Assets/Scenes/Main.unity).

Harvesting now:

- Changes mature wheat to the harvested patch sprite.
- Creates a visible wheat bundle above the patch.
- Animates the bundle with a short hop.
- After 0.8 seconds, adds two wheat to grain storage and destroys the bundle.
- Returns the patch visual to empty after 0.35 seconds.

The visuals and reward settings are configurable in [FarmPatchTileFactorySO.cs](./Assets/Scripts/Tiles/Factories/FarmPatchTileFactorySO.cs:11) and wired in [Factory_FarmPatch.asset](./Assets/Database/Tiles/Factory_FarmPatch.asset).

Unity compiled and imported the scripts, asset, and scene without errors.

> Voidpet Game Developer Challenge
>
> Context
>
> At Voidpet, we are looking for a game developer who can take ownership of an entire game: gameplay, UI, systems, tooling, polish, and shipping.
>
> We work AI-first. We expect a strong developer to use coding agents and generative tools aggressively, establish a playable foundation quickly, recognize where the generated result falls short, and apply human judgment where it matters most.
>
> This exercise is designed to show us how you operate when given a substantial but recognizable game reference.
>
> Your Task
>
> Build a small, playable recreation of the core Hay Day experience for mobile.
>
> We are deliberately not providing a fixed feature checklist. Decide what part of Hay Day is most essential and what scope will best demonstrate your ability. We are not asking for the entire game.
>
> Use any engine or stack you like. Note that we ship with Unity, so a Unity submission is a strong plus.
>
> Your result should feel like a coherent miniature game rather than a collection of disconnected systems.
>
> AI Use Is Expected
>
> This is an AI-native assignment. You should use Codex, Claude Code, Cursor, Copilot, image-generation tools, or any other AI tools that help you move faster.
>
> We are not evaluating whether you manually wrote every line of code. We are evaluating whether you can:
>
> Give agents useful direction
>
> Decompose a large goal into effective steps
>
> Recognize and correct weak generated output
>
> Make good product and engineering decisions quickly
>
> Make it look and feel genuinely good — visual quality weighs heavily in our review
>
> Ship a working build
>
> A strong submission may begin with only a few substantial prompts followed by focused iteration and art or interaction polish.
>
> What to Submit
>
> Submit one GitHub repository URL. The repository must be accessible to us and contain your source code. Its README must include:
>
> A playable build link, or a downloadable build with run instructions
>
> A video of what you made
>
> Your AI session transcript, or a link to it
>
> Submit your GitHub repository
>
> Hi Jayben, thanks for applying to the Game Developer role at Voidpet.
>
